{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_01VKH8CCmmg8XeGNni4nqxPK","type":"tool_result","content":"{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\\n5: \\n6: ## Client Configuration\\n7: \\n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\\n13: \\n14: ## REST Client\\n15: \\n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\\n19: \\n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\\n23: is injected into requests.\\n24: \\n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\\n28: \\n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\\n30: \\n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \\n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\\n46: \\n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\\n48: \\n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\\n57: \\n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\\n59: \\n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\\n61: models.\\n62: \\n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\\n64: \\n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\\n71: \\n72: ## Code Generation\\n73: \\n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\\n77: several key components:\\n78: \\n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\\n83: \\n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\\n86: \\n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\\n88: \\n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\\n90: \\n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\\n93: \\n94: The data flow is as follows:\\n95: \\n96: ```mermaid\\n97: graph TD\\n98:     subgraph \\\"Kubernetes API\\\"\\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\\n100:     end\\n101: \\n102:     subgraph \\\"client-go: Informer Mechanism\\\"\\n103:         Reflector(\\\"1. Reflector\\\")\\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\\\"2. DeltaFIFO\\\")\\n105:         Indexer[\\\"3. Indexer (Cache)\\\"]\\n106:         EventHandlers(\\\"4. Event Handlers\\\")\\n107:     end\\n108: \\n109:     subgraph \\\"User Code\\\"\\n110:         WorkQueue[\\\"5. Work Queue\\\"]\\n111:         Controller(\\\"6. Controller\\\")\\n112:     end\\n113: \\n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\\n121: ```\\n122: \\n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\\n126: **\\\"too old\\\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\\n129: \\n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\\n132: \\n133: ## Controller Patterns\\n134: \\n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\\n136: ensure resiliency.\\n137: \\n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\\n142: \\n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\\n145: shared `Lease` object.\\n146: \\n147: ## Server-Side Apply\\n148: \\n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\\n153: construct the declarative patch.\\n154: \\n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\\n156: \\n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\\n159: \\n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\\n163: \",\"url\":\"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md\"}"}]},"parent_tool_use_id":null,"session_id":"1b7329e0-cdfe-45e7-a16d-6c21fbeb35bc","uuid":"bb81ccfa-30e5-468c-94eb-d9478ed11c84","tool_use_result":{"content":"{\"content\":\"1: # `client-go` Architecture\\n2: \\n3: This document explains the internal architecture of `client-go` for contributors. It describes the\n4: major components, how they interact, and the key design decisions that shape the library.\n5: \n6: ## Client Configuration\n7: \n8: There is an architectural separation between loading client configuration and using it. The\n9: `rest.Config` object is the in-memory representation of this configuration. The\n10: `tools/clientcmd` package is the standard factory for producing it. `clientcmd` handles the\n11: complex logic of parsing `kubeconfig` files, merging contexts, and handling external\n12: authentication providers (e.g., OIDC).\n13: \n14: ## REST Client\n15: \n16: The `rest.Client` is the foundational HTTP client that underpins all other clients. It separates\n17: the low-level concerns of HTTP transport, serialization, and error handling from higher-level,\n18: Kubernetes-specific object logic.\n19: \n20: The `rest.Config` object is used to build the underlying HTTP transport, which is typically a\n21: chain of `http.RoundTripper` objects. Each element in the chain is responsible for a specific\n22: task, such as adding an `Authorization` header. This is the mechanism by which all authentication\n23: is injected into requests.\n24: \n25: The client uses a builder pattern for requests (e.g., `.Verb()`, `.Resource()`), deferring\n26: response processing until a method like `.Into(&pod)` is called. This separation is key to\n27: supporting different client models from a common base.\n28: \n29: ### Endpoint Interactions\n30: \n31: *   **Content Negotiation:** The client uses HTTP `Accept` headers to negotiate the wire format\n32:     (JSON or Protobuf). A key performance optimization using this mechanism is the ability to\n33:     request metadata-only objects via the `as=PartialObjectMetadata;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameter.\n34:     Also the `as=Table;g=meta.k8s.io;v=v1` Accept custom parameters may be used to request lists as tables. \n35: *   **Subresources:** The client can target standard subresources like `/status` or `/scale` for\n36:     object mutations, and it can also handle action-oriented subresources like `/logs` or\n37:     `/exec`, which often involve streaming data.\n38: *   **List Pagination:** For `LIST` requests, the client can specify a `limit`. The server will\n39:     return up to that many items and, if more exist, a `continue` token. The client is\n40:     responsible for passing this token in a subsequent request to retrieve the next page.\n41:     Higher-level tools like the `Reflector`'s `ListerWatcher` handle this logic automatically.\n42: *   **Streaming Watches:** A `WATCH` request returns a `watch.Interface` (from\n43:     `k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/watch`), which provides a channel of structured `watch.Event`\n44:     objects (`ADDED`, `MODIFIED`, `DELETED`, `BOOKMARK`). This decouples the watch consumer from\n45:     the underlying streaming protocol.\n46: \n47: ### Errors, Warnings, and Rate Limiting\n48: \n49: *   **Structured Errors:** The client deserializes non-2xx responses into a structured\n50:     `errors.StatusError`, enabling programmatic error handling (e.g., `errors.IsNotFound(err)`).\n51: *   **Warnings:** It processes non-fatal `Warning` headers from the API server via a\n52:     `WarningHandler`.\n53: *   **Client-Side Rate Limiting:** The `QPS` and `Burst` settings in `rest.Config` are the\n54:     client's half of the contract with the server's API Priority and Fairness system.\n55: *   **Server-Side Throttling:** The client's default transport automatically handles HTTP `429`\n56:     responses by reading the `Retry-After` header, waiting, and retrying the request.\n57: \n58: ## Typed and Dynamic Clients\n59: \n60: To handle the extensible nature of the Kubernetes API, `client-go` provides two primary client\n61: models.\n62: \n63: The **`kubernetes.Clientset`** provides compile-time, type-safe access to core, built-in APIs.\n64: \n65: The **`dynamic.DynamicClient`** represents all objects as `unstructured.Unstructured`, allowing it\n66: to interact with any API resource, including CRDs. It relies on two discovery mechanisms:\n67: 1.  The **`discovery.DiscoveryClient`** determines *what* resources exist. The\n68:     **`CachedDiscoveryClient`** is an optimization that caches this data on disk to solve.\n69: 2.  The **OpenAPI schema** (fetched from `/openapi/v3`) describes the *structure* of those\n70:     resources, providing the schema awareness needed by the dynamic client.\n71: \n72: ## Code Generation\n73: \n74: A core architectural principle of `client-go` is the use of code generation to provide a\n75: strongly-typed, compile-time-safe interface for specific API GroupVersions. This makes\n76: controller code more robust and easier to maintain. The tools in `k8s.io/code-generator` produce\n77: several key components:\n78: \n79: *   **Typed Clientsets:** The primary interface for interacting with a specific GroupVersion.\n80: *   **Typed Listers:** The read-only, cached accessors used by controllers.\n81: *   **Typed Informers:** The machinery for populating the cache for a specific type.\n82: *   **Apply Configurations:** The type-safe builders for Server-Side Apply.\n83: \n84: A contributor modifying a built-in API type **must** run the code generation scripts to update all\n85: of these dependent components. For the Kubernetes project, `hack/update-codegen.sh` runs code generation.\n86: \n87: `sample-controller` shows how code generate can be configured to build custom controllers.\n88: \n89: ## Controller Infrastructure\n90: \n91: The `tools/cache` package provides the core infrastructure for controllers, replacing a high-load,\n92: request-based pattern with a low-load, event-driven, cached model.\n93: \n94: The data flow is as follows:\n95: \n96: ```mermaid\n97: graph TD\n98:     subgraph \"Kubernetes API\"\n99:         API_Server[API Server]\n100:     end\n101: \n102:     subgraph \"client-go: Informer Mechanism\"\n103:         Reflector(\"1. Reflector\")\n104:         DeltaFIFO(\"2. DeltaFIFO\")\n105:         Indexer[\"3. Indexer (Cache)\"]\n106:         EventHandlers(\"4. Event Handlers\")\n107:     end\n108: \n109:     subgraph \"User Code\"\n110:         WorkQueue[\"5. Work Queue\"]\n111:         Controller(\"6. Controller\")\n112:     end\n113: \n114:     API_Server -- LIST/WATCH --> Reflector\n115:     Reflector -- Puts changes into --> DeltaFIFO\n116:     DeltaFIFO -- Is popped by internal loop, which updates --> Indexer\n117:     Indexer -- Update triggers --> EventHandlers\n118:     EventHandlers -- Adds key to --> WorkQueue\n119:     WorkQueue -- Is processed by --> Controller\n120:     Controller -- Reads from cache via Lister --> Indexer\n121: ```\n122: \n123: A **`Reflector`** performs a `LIST` to get a consistent snapshot of a resource, identified by a\n124: `resourceVersion`. It then starts a `WATCH` from that `resourceVersion` to receive a continuous\n125: stream of subsequent changes. The `Reflector`'s relist/rewatch loop is designed to solve the\n126: **\"too old\" `resourceVersion` error** by re-listing. To make this recovery more efficient, the\n127: `Reflector` consumes **watch bookmarks** from the server, which provide a more recent\n128: `resourceVersion` to restart from.\n129: \n130: The **`Lister`** is the primary, read-only, thread-safe interface for a controller's business\n131: logic to access the `Indexer`'s cache.\n132: \n133: ## Controller Patterns\n134: \n135: The controller infrastructure is architecturally decoupled from the controller's business logic to\n136: ensure resiliency.\n137: \n138: The **`util/workqueue`** creates a critical boundary between event detection (the informer's job)\n139: and reconciliation (the controller's job). Informer event handlers only add an object's key to the\n140: work queue. This allows the controller to retry failed operations with exponential backoff without\n141: blocking the informer's watch stream.\n142: \n143: For high availability, the **`tools/leaderelection`** package provides the standard architectural\n144: solution to ensure single-writer semantics by having replicas compete to acquire a lock on a\n145: shared `Lease` object.\n146: \n147: ## Server-Side Apply\n148: \n149: `client-go` provides a distinct architectural pattern for object mutation that aligns with the\n150: server's declarative model. This is a separate workflow from the traditional `get-modify-update`\n151: model that allows multiple controllers to safely co-manage the same object. The\n152: **`applyconfigurations`** package provides the generated, type-safe builder API used to\n153: construct the declarative patch.\n154: \n155: ## Versioning and Compatibility\n156: \n157: `client-go` has a strict versioning relationship with the main Kubernetes repository. A `client-go`\n158: version `v0.X.Y` corresponds to the Kubernetes version `v1.X.Y`.\n159: \n160: The Kubernetes API has strong backward compatibility guarantees: a client built with an older\n161: version of `client-go` will work with a newer API server. However, the reverse is not guaranteed.\n162: A contributor must not break compatibility with supported versions of the Kubernetes API server.\n163: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/ARCHITECTURE.md"}}}

The server should return a decodable API resource\n82: // object, or an api.Status object which contains information about the reason for\n83: // any failure.\n84: //\n85: // Most consumers should use client.New() to get a Kubernetes API client.\n86: type RESTClient struct {\n87: \t// base is the root URL for all invocations of the client\n88: \tbase *url.URL\n89: \t// versionedAPIPath is a path segment connecting the base URL to the resource root\n90: \tversionedAPIPath string\n91: \n92: \t// content describes how a RESTClient encodes and decodes responses.\n93: \tcontent requestClientContentConfigProvider\n94: \n95: \t// creates BackoffManager that is passed to requests.\n96: \tcreateBackoffMgr func() BackoffManagerWithContext\n97: \n98: \t// rateLimiter is shared among all requests created by this client unless specifically\n99: \t// overridden.\n100: \trateLimiter flowcontrol.RateLimiter\n101: \n102: \t// warningHandler is shared among all requests created by this client.\n103: \t// If not set, defaultWarningHandler is used.\n104: \twarningHandler WarningHandlerWithContext\n105: \n106: \t// Set specific behavior of the client. All are\n32: // optional.\n33: type DeltaFIFOOptions struct {\n34: \n35: \t// KeyFunction is used to figure out what key an object should have. (It's\n36: \t// exposed in the returned DeltaFIFO's KeyOf() method, with additional\n37: \t// handling around deleted objects and queue state).\n38: \t// Optional, the default is MetaNamespaceKeyFunc.\n39: \tKeyFunction KeyFunc\n40: \n41: \t// KnownObjects is expected to return a list of keys that the consumer of\n42: \t// this queue \"knows about\". It is used to decide which items are missing\n43: \t// when Replace() is called; 'Deleted' deltas are produced for the missing items.\n44: \t// KnownObjects may be nil if you can tolerate missing deletions on Replace().\n45: \tKnownObjects KeyListerGetter\n46: \n47: \t// EmitDeltaTypeReplaced indicates that the queue consumer\n48: \t// understands the Replaced DeltaType. Before the `Replaced` event type was\n49: \t// added, calls to Replace() were handled the same as Sync(). For\n50: \t// backwards-compatibility purposes, this is false by default.\n51: \t// When true, `Replaced` events will be sent for items passed to a Replace() call.\n52: \t// When false, `Sync` events will be sent instead.\n53: \tEmitDeltaTypeReplaced bool\n54: \n55: \t// If set, will be called for objects before enqueueing them. Please\n56: \t// see the comment on TransformFunc for details.\n57: \tTransformer TransformFunc\n58: \n59: \t// If set, log output will go to this logger instead of klog.Background().\n60: \tLogger *klog.Logger\n61: }\n62: \n63: // DeltaFIFO is like FIFO, but differs in two ways.  One is that the\n64: // accumulator associated with a given object's key is not that object\n65: // but rather a Deltas, which is a slice of Delta values for that\n66: // object.  Applying an object to a Deltas means to append a Delta\n67: // except when the potentially appended Delta is a Deleted and the\n68: // Deltas already ends with a Deleted.  In that case the Deltas does\n69: // not grow, although the terminal Deleted will be replaced by the new\n70: // Deleted if the older Deleted's object is a\n71: // DeletedFinalStateUnknown.\n72: //\n73: // The other difference is that DeltaFIFO has two additional ways that\n74: // an object can be applied to an accumulator: Replaced and Sync.\n75: // If EmitDeltaTypeReplaced is not set to true, Sync will be used in\n76: // replace events for backwards compatibility.  Sync is used for periodic\n77: // resync events.\n78: //\n79: // DeltaFIFO is a producer-consumer queue, where a Reflector is\n80: // intended to be the producer, and the consumer is whatever calls\n81: // the Pop() method.\n82: //\n83: // DeltaFIFO solves this use case:\n84: //   - You want to process every object change (delta) at most once.\n85: //   - When you process an object, you want to see everything\n86: //     that's happened to it since you last processed it.\n87: //   - You want to process the deletion of some of the objects.\n88: //   - You might want to periodically reprocess objects.\n89: //\n90: // DeltaFIFO's Pop(), Get(), and GetByKey() methods return\n91: // interface{} to satisfy the Store/Queue interfaces, but they\n92: // will always return an object of type Deltas. List() returns\n93: // the newest object from each accumulator in the FIFO.\n94: //\n95: // A DeltaFIFO's knownObjects KeyListerGetter provides the abilities\n96: // to list Store keys and to get objects by Store key.  The objects in\n97: // question are called \"known objects\" and this set of objects\n98: // modifies the behavior of the Delete, Replace, and Resync methods\n99: // (each in a different way).\n100: //\n101: // A note on threading: If you call Pop() in parallel from multiple\n102: // threads, you could end up with multiple threads processing slightly\n103: // different versions of the same An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\\n50: //\\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \\\"absent\\\".\\n55: //\\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\\n68: // selector.\\n69: //\\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\\n76: //\\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\\n78: // during `Run()`.\\n79: //\\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\\n87: //\\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\\n94: //\\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\\n99: // into its constituent parts.\\n100: //\\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\\n104: //\\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n130: //\\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n133: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n134: // to something else, for example through a\\n135: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n136: //\\n137: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n138: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n139: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n140: type SharedInformer interface {\\n141: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n142: \\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\\n143: \\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\\n144: \\t// different handlers.\\n145: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\\n146: \\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\\n147: \\t// seen every item in the initial list).\\n148: \\t//\\n149: \\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\\n150: \\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\\n151: It is possible that additional cache\\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\\n130: //\\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\\n133: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\\n134: // to something else, for example through a\\n135: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\\n136: //\\n137: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\\n138: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\\n139: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\\n140: type SharedInformer interface {\\n141: \\t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\\n142: \\t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\\n143: \\t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\\n144: \\t// different handlers.\\n145: \\t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\\n146: \\t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\\n147: \\t// seen every item in the initial list).\\n148: \\t//\\n149: \\t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\\n150: \\tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\\n151: and\n14: limitations under the License.\n15: */\n16: \n17: package cache\n18: \n19: import (\n20: \t\"context\"\n21: \t\"errors\"\n22: \t\"fmt\"\n23: \t\"sync\"\n24: \t\"sync/atomic\"\n25: \t\"time\"\n26: \n27: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/api/meta\"\n28: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/runtime\"\n29: \tutilruntime \"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/runtime\"\n30: \t\"k8s.io/apimachinery/pkg/util/wait\"\n31: \t\"k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/synctrack\"\n32: \t\"k8s.io/utils/buffer\"\n33: \t\"k8s.io/utils/clock\"\n34: \t\"k8s.io/utils/ptr\"\n35: \n36: \t\"k8s.io/klog/v2\"\n37: \n38: \tclientgofeaturegate \"k8s.io/client-go/features\"\n39: )\n40: \n41: // SharedInformer provides eventually consistent linkage of its\n42: // clients to the authoritative state of a given collection of\n43: // objects.  An object is identified by its API group, kind/resource,\n44: // namespace (if any), and name; the `ObjectMeta.UID` is not part of\n45: // an object's ID as far as this contract is concerned.  One\n46: // SharedInformer provides linkage to objects of a particular API\n47: // group and kind/resource.  The linked object collection of a\n48: // SharedInformer may be further restricted to one namespace (if\n49: // applicable) and/or by label selector and/or field selector.\n50: //\n51: // The authoritative state of an object is what apiservers provide\n52: // access to, and an object goes through a strict sequence of states.\n53: // An object state is either (1) present with a ResourceVersion and\n54: // other appropriate content or (2) \"absent\".\n55: //\n56: // A SharedInformer maintains a local cache --- exposed by GetStore(),\n57: // by GetIndexer() in the case of an indexed informer, and possibly by\n58: // machinery involved in creating and/or accessing the informer --- of\n59: // the state of each relevant object.  This cache is eventually\n60: // consistent with the authoritative state.  This means that, unless\n61: // prevented by persistent communication problems, if ever a\n62: // particular object ID X is authoritatively associated with a state S\n63: // then for every SharedInformer I whose collection includes (X, S)\n64: // eventually either (1) I's cache associates X with S or a later\n65: // state of X, (2) I is stopped, or (3) the authoritative state\n66: // service for X terminates.  To be formally complete, we say that the\n67: // absent state meets any restriction by label selector or field\n68: // selector.\n69: //\n70: // For a given informer and relevant object ID X, the sequence of\n71: // states that appears in the informer's cache is a subsequence of the\n72: // states authoritatively associated with X.  That is, some states\n73: // might never appear in the cache but ordering among the appearing\n74: // states is correct.  Note, however, that there is no promise about\n75: // ordering between states seen for different objects.\n76: //\n77: // The local cache starts out empty, and gets populated and updated\n78: // during `Run()`.\n79: //\n80: // As a simple example, if a collection of objects is henceforth\n81: // unchanging, a SharedInformer is created that links to that\n82: // collection, and that SharedInformer is `Run()` then that\n83: // SharedInformer's cache eventually holds an exact copy of that\n84: // collection (unless it is stopped too soon, the authoritative state\n85: // service ends, or communication problems between the two\n86: // persistently thwart achievement).\n87: //\n88: // As another simple example, if the local cache ever holds a\n89: // non-absent state for some object ID and the object is eventually\n90: // removed from the authoritative state then eventually the object is\n91: // removed from the local cache (unless the SharedInformer is stopped\n92: // too soon, the authoritative state service ends, or communication\n93: // problems persistently thwart the desired result).\n94: //\n95: // The keys in the Store are of the form namespace/name for namespaced\n96: // objects, and are simply the name for non-namespaced objects.\n97: // Clients can use `MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)` to extract the key for\n98: // a given object, and `SplitMetaNamespaceKey(key)` to split a key\n99: // into its constituent parts.\n100: //\n101: // Every query against the local cache is answered entirely from one\n102: // snapshot of the cache's state.  Thus, the result of a `List` call\n103: // will not contain two entries with the same namespace and name.\n104: //\n105: // A client is identified here by a ResourceEventHandler.  For every\n106: // update to the SharedInformer's local cache and for every client\n107: // added before `Run()`, eventually either the SharedInformer is\n108: // stopped or the client is notified of the update.  A client added\n109: // after `Run()` starts gets a startup batch of notifications of\n110: // additions of the objects existing in the cache at the time that\n111: // client was added; also, for every update to the SharedInformer's\n112: // local cache after that client was added, eventually either the\n113: // SharedInformer is stopped or that client is notified of that\n114: // update.  Client notifications happen after the corresponding cache\n115: // update and, in the case of a SharedIndexInformer, after the\n116: // corresponding index updates.  It is possible that additional cache\n117: // and index updates happen before such a prescribed notification.\n118: // For a given SharedInformer and client, the notifications are\n119: // delivered sequentially.  For a given SharedInformer, client, and\n120: // object ID, the notifications are delivered in order.  Because\n121: // `ObjectMeta.UID` has no role in identifying objects, it is possible\n122: // that when (1) object O1 with ID (e.g. namespace and name) X and\n123: // `ObjectMeta.UID` U1 in the SharedInformer's local cache is deleted\n124: // and later (2) another object O2 with ID X and ObjectMeta.UID U2 is\n125: // created the informer's clients are not notified of (1) and (2) but\n126: // rather are notified only of an update from O1 to O2. Clients that\n127: // need to detect such cases might do so by comparing the `ObjectMeta.UID`\n128: // field of the old and the new object in the code that handles update\n129: // notifications (i.e. `OnUpdate` method of ResourceEventHandler).\n130: //\n131: // A client must process each notification promptly; a SharedInformer\n132: // is not engineered to deal well with a large backlog of\n133: // notifications to deliver.  Lengthy processing should be passed off\n134: // to something else, for example through a\n135: // `client-go/util/workqueue`.\n136: //\n137: // A delete notification exposes the last locally known non-absent\n138: // state, except that its ResourceVersion is replaced with a\n139: // ResourceVersion in which the object is actually absent.\n140: type SharedInformer interface {\n141: \t// AddEventHandler adds an event handler to the shared informer using\n142: \t// the shared informer's resync period.  Events to a single handler are\n143: \t// delivered sequentially, but there is no coordination between\n144: \t// different handlers.\n145: \t// It returns a registration handle for the handler that can be used to\n146: \t// remove the handler again, or to tell if the handler is synced (has\n147: \t// seen every item in the initial list).\n148: \t//\n149: \t// Contextual logging: AddEventHandlerWithOptions together with a logger in the options should be used instead of AddEventHandler in code which supports contextual logging.\n150: \tAddEventHandler(handler ResourceEventHandler) (ResourceEventHandlerRegistration, error)\n151: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/tools/cache/shared_informer.go%3FL1-150"}}}

In this controller it simply prints\n74: // information about the pod to stdout. In case an error happened, it has to simply return the error.\n75: // The retry logic should not be part of the business logic.\n76: func (c *Controller) syncToStdout(key string) error {\n77: \tobj, exists, err := c.indexer.GetByKey(key)\n78: \tif err != nil {\n79: \t\tklog.Errorf(\"Fetching object with key %s from store failed with %v\", key, err)\n80: \t\treturn err\n81: \t}\n82: \n83: \tif !exists {\n84: \t\t// Below we will warm up our cache with a Pod, so that we will see a delete for one pod\n85: \t\tfmt.Printf(\"Pod %s does not exist anymore\\n\", key)\n86: \t} else {\n87: \t\t// Note that you also have to check the uid if you have a local controlled resource, which\n88: \t\t// is dependent on the actual instance, to detect that a Pod was recreated with the same name\n89: \t\tfmt.Printf(\"Sync/Add/Update for Pod %s\\n\", obj.(*v1.Pod).GetName())\n90: \t}\n91: \treturn nil\n92: }\n93: \n94: // handleErr checks if an error happened and makes sure we will retry later.\n95: func (c *Controller) handleErr(err error, key string) {\n96: \tif err == nil {\n97: \t\t// Forget about the #AddRateLimited history of the key on every successful synchronization.\n98: \t\t// This ensures that future processing of updates for this key is not delayed because of\n99: \t\t// an outdated error history.\n100: \t\tc.queue.Forget(key)\n101: \t\treturn\n102: \t}\n103: \n104: \t// This controller retries 5 times if something goes wrong. This way we make sure that\n178: \t// whenever the cache is updated, the pod key is added to the workqueue.\n179: \t// Note that when we finally process the item from the workqueue, we might see a newer version\n180: \t// of the Pod than the version which was responsible for triggering the update.\n181: \tindexer, informer := cache.NewIndexerInformer(podListWatcher, &v1.Pod{}, 0, cache.ResourceEventHandlerFuncs{\n182: \t\tAddFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n183: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n184: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n185: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n186: \t\t\t}\n187: \t\t},\n188: \t\tUpdateFunc: func(old interface{}, new interface{}) {\n189: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.MetaNamespaceKeyFunc(new)\n190: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n191: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n192: \t\t\t}\n193: \t\t},\n194: \t\tDeleteFunc: func(obj interface{}) {\n195: \t\t\t// IndexerInformer uses a delta queue, therefore for deletes we have to use this\n196: \t\t\t// key function.\n197: \t\t\tkey, err := cache.DeletionHandlingMetaNamespaceKeyFunc(obj)\n198: \t\t\tif err == nil {\n199: \t\t\t\tqueue.Add(key)\n200: \t\t\t}\n201: ","url":"https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-evals/kubernetes--8c9c67c0@8c9c67c000104450cfc5a5f48053a9a84b73cf93/-/blob/staging/src/k8s.io/client-go/examples/workqueue/main.go%3FL1-200"}}}
